<p><b>Millard Fillmore</b> (January 7, 1800&#160;– March 8, 1874) was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">13th President of the United States</a> (1850–53), the last to be a member of the <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig Party</a> while in the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>. A former congressman from New York, Fillmore was elected the nation's 12th <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President</a> in 1848, and was elevated to the presidency by the death of <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">Zachary Taylor</a>. He was instrumental in getting the <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1850" title="Compromise of 1850">Compromise of 1850</a> passed, a bargain that led to a brief truce in the battle over <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">slavery</a>. He failed to win the Whig nomination for president in 1852; he gained the endorsement of the <a href="/wiki/Nativism_(politics)" title="Nativism (politics)">nativist</a> <a href="/wiki/Know_Nothing_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Know Nothing Party">Know Nothing Party</a> four years later, and finished third in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1856" title="United States presidential election, 1856">that election</a>.</p>
<p>Fillmore was born into poverty in the <a href="/wiki/Finger_Lakes" title="Finger Lakes">Finger Lakes</a> area of <a href="/wiki/New_York_state" class="mw-redirect" title="New York state">New York state</a>; his parents were tenant farmers during his formative years. He rose from poverty through study, and became a lawyer though he had little formal schooling. He became prominent in the <a href="/wiki/Buffalo,_New_York" title="Buffalo, New York">Buffalo</a> area as an attorney and politician, was elected to the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Assembly">New York Assembly</a> in 1828, and to the <a href="/wiki/U.S._House_of_Representatives" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. House of Representatives">U.S. House of Representatives</a> in 1832. Initially, he belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Masonic_Party" title="Anti-Masonic Party">Anti-Masonic Party</a>, but became a Whig as the party formed in the mid-1830s; he was a rival for state party leadership with editor <a href="/wiki/Thurlow_Weed" title="Thurlow Weed">Thurlow Weed</a> and Weed's protégé, <a href="/wiki/William_H._Seward" title="William H. Seward">William H. Seward</a>. Through his career, Fillmore decried slavery as an evil, but one beyond the powers of the federal government, whereas Seward was not only openly hostile to slavery, he argued that the federal government had a role to play in ending it. Fillmore was an unsuccessful candidate for <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">Speaker of the House</a> when the Whigs took control of the chamber in 1841, but was made <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Ways_and_Means" title="United States House Committee on Ways and Means">Ways and Means Committee</a> chairman. Defeated in bids for the Whig nomination for vice president in 1844, and for New York governor the same year, Fillmore was elected <a href="/wiki/Comptroller_of_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Comptroller of New York">Comptroller of New York</a> in 1847, the first to hold that post by direct election.</p>
<p>Fillmore received the Whig vice presidential nomination in 1848 as Taylor's running mate, and the two were elected. He was largely ignored by Taylor, including the dispensing of patronage in New York, on which Taylor consulted Weed and Seward. As vice president, Fillmore presided over angry debates in the Senate as Congress decided whether to allow slavery in the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Cession" title="Mexican Cession">Mexican Cession</a>. Fillmore supported <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>'s Omnibus Bill (the basis of the 1850 Compromise) though Taylor did not. After President Taylor died in July 1850, Fillmore dismissed the cabinet and changed the administration's policy. The new president exerted pressure to gain the passage of the Compromise, which gave legislative victories to both North and South, and which was enacted by September. The <a href="/wiki/Fugitive_Slave_Act_of_1850" title="Fugitive Slave Act of 1850">Fugitive Slave Act</a>, expediting the return of escaped slaves to those who claimed ownership, was a controversial part of the Compromise, and Fillmore felt himself duty-bound to enforce it, though it damaged his popularity and also the Whig Party, which was torn North from South. In foreign policy, Fillmore supported <a href="/wiki/Perry_Expedition" title="Perry Expedition">U.S. Navy expeditions to open trade in Japan</a>, opposed <a href="/wiki/Second_French_Empire" title="Second French Empire">French</a> designs on Hawaii, and was embarrassed by <a href="/wiki/Narciso_L%C3%B3pez" title="Narciso López">Narciso López's</a> filibuster expeditions to Cuba. He sought election to a full term in 1852, but was passed over by the Whigs in favor of <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a>.</p>
<p>As the Whig Party broke up after Fillmore's presidency, many of Fillmore's conservative wing joined the Know Nothings, forming the American Party. In his 1856 candidacy as that party's nominee, Fillmore had little to say about immigration, instead focusing on the preservation of the Union, and won only Maryland. In his retirement, Fillmore was active in many civic endeavors. He helped to found the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Buffalo" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Buffalo">University of Buffalo</a>, serving as its first chancellor. During the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, Fillmore denounced secession and agreed that the Union must be maintained by force if necessary, but was critical of the war policies of <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>. After peace was restored, he supported the <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_era_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Reconstruction era of the United States">Reconstruction</a> policies of President <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a>. Obscure today, Fillmore has been praised by some for his foreign policy, but he is criticized by others for his enforcement of the Fugitive Slave Act and for his association with the Know Nothings.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_career">Early life and career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Millard_Fillmore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fillmorebirth.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Fillmorebirth.jpg/220px-Fillmorebirth.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Fillmorebirth.jpg/330px-Fillmorebirth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Fillmorebirth.jpg/440px-Fillmorebirth.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fillmorebirth.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Historical marker at the site of Fillmore's birth</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Millard Fillmore was born in a <a href="/wiki/Log_cabin" title="Log cabin">log cabin</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[b]</a></sup> on a farm in what is now <a href="/wiki/Moravia,_New_York" title="Moravia, New York">Moravia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cayuga_County,_New_York" title="Cayuga County, New York">Cayuga County</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Finger_Lakes" title="Finger Lakes">Finger Lakes</a> region of <a href="/wiki/New_York_state" class="mw-redirect" title="New York state">New York state</a>, on January 7, 1800. His parents were Phoebe (Millard) and <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Fillmore" title="Nathaniel Fillmore">Nathaniel Fillmore</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmerican_National_Biography_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmerican_National_Biography-4">[2]</a></sup> He was the second of eight children and the oldest son.<sup id="cite_ref-pre_president_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pre_president-5">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nathaniel Fillmore was the son of Nathaniel Fillmore Sr. (1739-1814), a native of <a href="/wiki/Franklin,_Connecticut" title="Franklin, Connecticut">Franklin, Connecticut</a> who became one of the earliest settlers of <a href="/wiki/Bennington,_Vermont" title="Bennington, Vermont">Bennington</a> when it was founded in the territory then called the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Grants" title="New Hampshire Grants">New Hampshire Grants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[4]</a></sup> Nathaniel Fillmore Sr. was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Green_Mountain_Boys" title="Green Mountain Boys">Green Mountain Boys</a>, and served as an <a href="/wiki/Ensign_(rank)#United_States" title="Ensign (rank)">ensign</a> and <a href="/wiki/First_lieutenant#U.S._Army.2C_U.S._Marine_Corps_and_U.S._Air_Force" title="First lieutenant">first lieutenant</a> during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Lineage_Book.2C_National_Society_of_the_Daughters_of_the_American_Revolution.27.27_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Lineage_Book.2C_National_Society_of_the_Daughters_of_the_American_Revolution.27.27-7">[5]</a></sup> In 1767, Nathaniel Fillmore Sr. married Hepzibah Wood (1747-1783), the mother of Nathaniel Fillmore and grandmother of Millard Fillmore.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Lineage_Book.2C_National_Society_of_the_Daughters_of_the_American_Revolution.27.27_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Lineage_Book.2C_National_Society_of_the_Daughters_of_the_American_Revolution.27.27-7">[5]</a></sup> Members of the Fillmore family had resided in <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> for several generations; Nathaniel Fillmore Sr. was the son of John Fillmore (1702-1777), who lived in <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, and worked as a ship's captain.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[7]</a></sup> John Fillmore was the son of John Fillmore Sr. (1676-1712), a native of <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> who was also a mariner and died as a prisoner of the French on the island of <a href="/wiki/Martinique" title="Martinique">Martinique</a> during <a href="/wiki/Queen_Anne%27s_War" title="Queen Anne's War">Queen Anne's War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Memorials_of_the_Family_of_Fynmore.27.27_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Memorials_of_the_Family_of_Fynmore.27.27-10">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nathaniel Fillmore and Phoebe Millard moved from Vermont in 1799, seeking better opportunities than were available on Nathaniel's stony farm, but the title to their Cayuga County land proved defective, and the Fillmore family moved to nearby <a href="/wiki/Sempronius,_New_York" title="Sempronius, New York">Sempronius</a>, where they leased land as tenant farmers, and Nathaniel occasionally taught school.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback191.E2.80.9397_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback191.E2.80.9397-11">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[10]</a></sup> As historian Tyler Anbinder described it, Fillmore's "childhood was one of hard work, frequent privation, and virtually no formal schooling".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmerican_National_Biography_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmerican_National_Biography-4">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Over time, Nathaniel Fillmore became more successful in Sempronius, but during Millard Fillmore's formative years the family experienced severe poverty. Eventually Nathaniel Fillmore became highly enough regarded that he was chosen to serve in local offices including justice of the peace.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup> Hoping his oldest son would learn a trade, when Millard was 14 his father talked him out of attempting to enlist for the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry18_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry18-14">[12]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Apprentice" class="mw-redirect" title="Apprentice">apprenticed</a> him to cloth maker Benjamin Hungerford in <a href="/wiki/Sparta,_New_York" title="Sparta, New York">Sparta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup> Fillmore was relegated to menial labor; unhappy at not learning any skills, he left Hungerford's employ.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry19_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry19-16">[14]</a></sup> His father then placed him in the same trade at a mill in <a href="/wiki/Niles,_New_York" title="Niles, New York">New Hope</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry20_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry20-17">[15]</a></sup> Seeking to better himself, Millard Fillmore bought a share in a circulating library, and read all the books he could.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry20_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry20-17">[15]</a></sup> In 1819, he took advantage of a time of little work at the mill to enroll at a new academy in the town, where he met a classmate, <a href="/wiki/Abigail_Fillmore" title="Abigail Fillmore">Abigail Powers</a>, and fell in love with her.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback224.E2.80.9358_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback224.E2.80.9358-18">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Later in 1819, Nathaniel moved the family to Montville, a hamlet of <a href="/wiki/Moravia,_New_York" title="Moravia, New York">Moravia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry22_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry22-19">[17]</a></sup> Appreciating his son's talents, Nathaniel Fillmore persuaded Judge Walter Wood, the Fillmores' landlord and the wealthiest person in the area, to allow Millard to be his law clerk for a trial period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry23_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry23-20">[18]</a></sup> Wood agreed to employ young Fillmore, and to supervise him as he <a href="/wiki/Read_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Read law">read law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry23_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry23-20">[18]</a></sup> Fillmore taught school for three months to earn money to buy out his apprenticeship at the mill.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry24_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry24-21">[19]</a></sup> Fillmore left Wood after 18 months; the judge paid him almost nothing, and the two quarreled when Fillmore earned a small sum by advising a farmer in a minor lawsuit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry25_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry25-22">[20]</a></sup> Refusing to pledge not to do it again, Fillmore left, abandoning his clerkship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback258.E2.80.93308_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback258.E2.80.93308-23">[21]</a></sup> Nathaniel Fillmore again moved the family, and Fillmore accompanied his father west to <a href="/wiki/East_Aurora,_New_York" title="East Aurora, New York">East Aurora</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Erie_County,_New_York" title="Erie County, New York">Erie County</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Buffalo,_New_York" title="Buffalo, New York">Buffalo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman5_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman5-24">[22]</a></sup> This move proved successful, and the farm Nathaniel Fillmore purchased there became prosperous.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> Nathaniel Fillmore would be the first father of a president to visit his son at the White House, and alluded to his family's onetime poverty when he told a questioner how to raise a son to be president: "Cradle him in a sap trough".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESnyder50_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnyder50-26">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1821, Fillmore turned 21 and was thus legally independent of his father.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry26_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry26-28">[26]</a></sup> He taught school in East Aurora, and accepted a few cases in justice of the peace courts, which did not require the practitioner to be a licensed attorney.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry26_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry26-28">[26]</a></sup> He moved to Buffalo the following year, continued his study of law, first while teaching school, and then in the law office of Asa Rice and Joseph Clary; he also became engaged to Abigail Powers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry26_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry26-28">[26]</a></sup> In 1823, he was <a href="/wiki/Bar_(law)" title="Bar (law)">admitted to the bar</a>, and declined offers from Buffalo law firms to return to East Aurora and establish a practice as the town's only lawyer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman5_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman5-24">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry528.E2.80.9334_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry528.E2.80.9334-29">[27]</a></sup> Later in life, Fillmore stated that he initially lacked the self-confidence to practice in the larger city of Buffalo; his biographer, Paul Finkelman, suggested that after being under others' thumbs all his life, Fillmore enjoyed the independence of his East Aurora practice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman5.E2.80.936_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman5.E2.80.936-30">[28]</a></sup> On February 5, 1826, Millard and Abigail wed. They had two children, <a href="/wiki/Millard_Powers_Fillmore" title="Millard Powers Fillmore">Millard Powers Fillmore</a> (1828–1889) and <a href="/wiki/Mary_Abigail_Fillmore" title="Mary Abigail Fillmore">Mary Abigail Fillmore</a> (1832–1854).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry128.E2.80.9334_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry128.E2.80.9334-31">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Buffalo_politician">Buffalo politician</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Millard_Fillmore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Buffalo politician">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fillmore_House.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Fillmore_House.jpg/220px-Fillmore_House.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Fillmore_House.jpg/330px-Fillmore_House.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Fillmore_House.jpg/440px-Fillmore_House.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fillmore_House.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Millard Fillmore helped build <a href="/wiki/Millard_Fillmore_House" class="mw-redirect" title="Millard Fillmore House">this house</a> in <a href="/wiki/East_Aurora,_New_York" title="East Aurora, New York">East Aurora, New York</a>, and lived here 1826–1830.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Members of the Fillmore family were active in politics and government; Millard Fillmore's grandfather, Nathaniel Fillmore Sr. served in local offices in Bennington including <a href="/wiki/Hayward_(profession)" title="Hayward (profession)">hayward</a>, highway surveyor, and tax collector.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> In addition to Fillmore's father's service as a justice of the peace, Fillmore's uncle <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Fillmore" title="Calvin Fillmore">Calvin Fillmore</a> served in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Assembly" title="New York State Assembly">New York State Assembly</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup> and another uncle, Simeon Fillmore, served as <a href="/wiki/Town_supervisor" title="Town supervisor">town supervisor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Clarence,_New_York" title="Clarence, New York">Clarence, New York</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Centennial_History_of_Erie_County.2C_New_York.27.27_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Centennial_History_of_Erie_County.2C_New_York.27.27-34">[32]</a></sup> Millard Fillmore was also interested in politics, and the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Masonic_Party" title="Anti-Masonic Party">Anti-Masonic Party</a> in the late 1820s provided his initial attraction and entry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman12.E2.80.9313_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman12.E2.80.9313-35">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many Anti-Masons were opposed to the presidential candidacy of General <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Mason_(Freemasonry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mason (Freemasonry)">Mason</a>, and Fillmore was a delegate to a New York convention that endorsed President <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a> for re-election, as well as serving at two Anti-Masonic conventions in the summer of 1828.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmerican_National_Biography_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmerican_National_Biography-4">[2]</a></sup> At the conventions, Fillmore and one of the early political bosses, newspaper editor <a href="/wiki/Thurlow_Weed" title="Thurlow Weed">Thurlow Weed</a>, met and impressed each other.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman12.E2.80.9313_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman12.E2.80.9313-35">[33]</a></sup> By then, Fillmore was the leading citizen in East Aurora, and successfully sought election to the <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Assembly" title="New York State Assembly">New York State Assembly</a>, serving in <a href="/wiki/Albany,_New_York" title="Albany, New York">Albany</a> for three one-year terms, 1829 to 1831.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmerican_National_Biography_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmerican_National_Biography-4">[2]</a></sup> Fillmore's 1828 election was in contrast to the victories of the <a href="/wiki/Jacksonian_democracy" title="Jacksonian democracy">Jacksonian Democrats</a> (soon the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrats</a>), who swept the general to the White House and their party to a majority in Albany, thus Fillmore was in the minority in the Assembly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry42_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry42-36">[34]</a></sup> He proved effective anyway, working to get the legislature to allow court witnesses the option of affirming to tell the truth rather than taking a religious oath, and in 1830 gaining the abolishment of <a href="/wiki/Imprisonment_for_debt" class="mw-redirect" title="Imprisonment for debt">imprisonment for debt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry18_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry18-14">[12]</a></sup> By then, much of Fillmore's legal practice was in Buffalo and he that year moved there with his family; he did not seek re-election in 1831.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith45_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith45-37">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman12.E2.80.9313_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman12.E2.80.9313-35">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fillmore also experienced success as a lawyer. Buffalo was then in a period of rapid expansion, recovering from being burned by the British during the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> to becoming the western terminus of the <a href="/wiki/Erie_Canal" title="Erie Canal">Erie Canal</a>. Cases from outside Erie County were already falling to Fillmore's lot, and he was prominent as a lawyer in Buffalo even before he moved there. He took <a href="/wiki/Nathan_K._Hall" title="Nathan K. Hall">Nathan K. Hall</a> as a law clerk in East Aurora; a lifelong friend, Hall would be Fillmore's partner in Buffalo and his <a href="/wiki/Postmaster_General_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Postmaster General of the United States">postmaster general</a> as president. Buffalo had been legally a village when Fillmore came there; although the bill to incorporate it as a city passed the legislature after Fillmore had left the Assembly, he helped draw up the city charter. In addition to his successful legal practice, Fillmore helped found the Buffalo High School Association, joined the <a href="/wiki/Lyceum" title="Lyceum">lyceum</a> and attended the local Unitarian church; he became one of the leading citizens of Buffalo.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback314.2C_750.E2.80.93810_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback314.2C_750.E2.80.93810-38">[36]</a></sup> He was also active in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Army_National_Guard" title="New York Army National Guard">New York Militia</a>, and attained the rank of <a href="/wiki/Major_(United_States)" title="Major (United States)">major</a> as inspector of the 47th Brigade.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Millard_Fillmore_Papers.27.27_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Millard_Fillmore_Papers.27.27-40">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Congressman">Congressman</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Millard_Fillmore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Congressman">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_term.3B_return_to_Buffalo">First term; return to Buffalo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Millard_Fillmore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: First term; return to Buffalo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although Fillmore had retired from the legislature after the 1831 session, he did not remain absent from politics for long. In 1832, he ran for the House of Representatives, and was elected. The Anti-Masonic presidential candidate, former Attorney General <a href="/wiki/William_Wirt_(Attorney_General)" title="William Wirt (Attorney General)">William Wirt</a>, won only Vermont as President Jackson easily gained re-election. At the time, Congress convened its annual session in December, and so Fillmore had to wait more than a year after his election to take his seat. Fillmore, Weed, and others had realized that opposition to Masonry was too narrow a foundation on which to build a national party, and assembled the broad-based <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig Party</a> from <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Party" title="National Republican Party">National Republicans</a>, Anti-Masons, and disaffected Democrats. The Whigs were initially united by their opposition to Jackson, but became a major party by expanding their platform to include support for economic growth through rechartering the <a href="/wiki/Second_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="Second Bank of the United States">Second Bank of the United States</a> and federally funded <a href="/wiki/Internal_improvements" title="Internal improvements">internal improvements</a> including roads, bridges, and canals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry936.E2.80.93940.2C_993.E2.80.93999_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry936.E2.80.93940.2C_993.E2.80.93999-41">[39]</a></sup> Weed joined the Whigs before Fillmore, and became a power within the party; his anti-slavery views were stronger than Fillmore's (who, though he disliked slavery, deemed the federal government without power over it), and closer to those of another prominent New York Whig, <a href="/wiki/William_H._Seward" title="William H. Seward">William H. Seward</a> of <a href="/wiki/Auburn,_New_York" title="Auburn, New York">Auburn</a>, who was also seen as a Weed protégé.<sup id="cite_ref-pre_president_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pre_president-5">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Washington, Fillmore urged the expansion of Buffalo harbor, a decision under federal jurisdiction, and in his private capacity served on a committee lobbying Albany for the expansion of the state-owned Erie Canal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback878.E2.80.93905_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback878.E2.80.93905-42">[40]</a></sup> Even during the 1832 campaign, Fillmore's affiliation as an Anti-Mason had been uncertain, and he rapidly shed the label once sworn in. Fillmore came to the notice of the influential Massachusetts senator <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Webster" title="Daniel Webster">Daniel Webster</a>, who took the new congressman under his wing. Fillmore became a firm supporter and the close relationship between the two would continue until Webster's death late in Fillmore's presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman13_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman13-43">[41]</a></sup> Despite Fillmore's support of the Second Bank as a means of national development, he did not speak in the congressional debates in which some advocated renewing its charter, although Jackson had previously vetoed legislation for a charter renewal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback1261_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback1261-44">[42]</a></sup> Fillmore supported building infrastructure, voting in favor of constructing a bridge across the <a href="/wiki/Potomac_River" title="Potomac River">Potomac River</a> and navigation improvements on the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">Hudson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry999_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry999-45">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Anti-Masonry was still strong in Western New York though it was petering out nationally, and when the Anti-Masons did not nominate him for a second term in 1834, Fillmore declined the Whig nomination, seeing that the two parties would split the anti-Jackson vote and elect the Democrat. Despite Fillmore's departure from office, he was a rival for state party leadership with Seward, the unsuccessful 1834 Whig gubernatorial candidate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman14_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman14-46">[44]</a></sup> Fillmore spent his time out of office building his law practice and boosting the Whig Party, which gradually absorbed most of the Anti-Masons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry1079_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry1079-47">[45]</a></sup> By 1836, Fillmore was confident enough of anti-Jackson unity that he accepted the Whig nomination for Congress. Democrats, led by their presidential candidate, Vice President <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a>, were victorious nationwide and in Van Buren's home state of New York, but Western New York voted Whig and sent Fillmore back to Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback1495.E2.80.931508_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback1495.E2.80.931508-48">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_through_fourth_terms">Second through fourth terms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Millard_Fillmore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Second through fourth terms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Van Buren, faced with the economic <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1837" title="Panic of 1837">Panic of 1837</a>, caused in part by lack of confidence in private bank note issues after Jackson had instructed the government <a href="/wiki/Specie_Circular" title="Specie Circular">to only accept gold or silver</a>, called a special session of Congress. Government money had been held in so-called "<a href="/wiki/Pet_banks" title="Pet banks">pet banks</a>" since Jackson had withdrawn it from the Second Bank; Van Buren proposed to place funds in sub-treasuries, government depositories that would not lend money. Believing that government funds should be lent to develop the country, Fillmore felt this would lock the nation's limited supply of gold money away from commerce. Van Buren's sub-treasury and other economic proposals passed, but as hard times continued, the Whigs saw an increased vote in the 1837 elections, and captured the New York Assembly. This set up a fight for the 1838 gubernatorial nomination. Fillmore supported the leading Whig vice presidential candidate from 1836, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Granger" title="Francis Granger">Francis Granger</a>; Weed preferred Seward. Fillmore was embittered when Weed got the nomination for Seward, but campaigned loyally; Seward was elected, while Fillmore won another term in the House.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback1556.E2.80.931679_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback1556.E2.80.931679-49">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rivalry between Fillmore and Seward was affected by the growing anti-slavery movement. Although Fillmore disliked slavery, he saw no reason it should be a political issue. Seward, on the other hand, was hostile to slavery and made that clear in his actions as governor, refusing to return slaves claimed by Southerners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback1556.E2.80.931679_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback1556.E2.80.931679-49">[47]</a></sup> When in 1839, the Buffalo bar proposed Fillmore for the position of vice chancellor of the eighth judicial district. Seward refused, and nominated <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Whittlesey" title="Frederick Whittlesey">Frederick Whittlesey</a>; in making the nomination, Seward indicated that if <a href="/wiki/New_York_Senate" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Senate">the state senate</a> rejected Whittlesey, he still would not appoint Fillmore.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry1326.E2.80.931331_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry1326.E2.80.931331-50">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fillmore was active in the discussions of presidential candidates that preceded the <a href="/wiki/1839_Whig_National_Convention" title="1839 Whig National Convention">Whig National Convention</a> for the 1840 race. He initially supported General <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a>, but really wanted to defeat Kentucky Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, a slaveholder he felt could not carry New York state. Fillmore did not attend the convention, but was gratified when it nominated General <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Harrison" title="William Henry Harrison">William Henry Harrison</a> for president, with former Virginia senator <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">John Tyler</a> his running mate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry1356.E2.80.931361_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry1356.E2.80.931361-51">[49]</a></sup> Fillmore organized Western New York for Harrison, and the national ticket was elected, while Fillmore easily gained a fourth term in the House.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry1891_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry1891-52">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the urging of Senator Clay, Harrison quickly called a special session of Congress. With the Whigs to organize the House for the first time, Fillmore sought the Speakership, but it went to a Clay acolyte, <a href="/wiki/John_White_(Kentucky_politician)" title="John White (Kentucky politician)">John White</a> of Kentucky.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback1950.E2.80.931957_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback1950.E2.80.931957-53">[51]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Fillmore was made chairman of the <a href="/wiki/House_Ways_and_Means_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="House Ways and Means Committee">House Ways and Means Committee</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmerican_National_Biography_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmerican_National_Biography-4">[2]</a></sup> Harrison was expected to go along with anything Clay and other congressional Whig leaders proposed, but died on April 4, 1841, elevating Vice President Tyler to the presidency. Tyler, a onetime maverick Democrat, soon broke with Clay over congressional proposals for a national bank to stabilize the currency, which he vetoed twice, leading to his expulsion from the Whig Party. Fillmore remained on the fringes of that conflict, generally supporting the congressional Whig position, but his chief achievement as Ways and Means chairman was the <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1842" title="Tariff of 1842">Tariff of 1842</a>. The existing tariff did not protect manufacturing, and part of the revenue was distributed to the states, a decision made in better times that was by then depleting the Treasury. Fillmore prepared a bill raising tariff rates that was popular in the country, but the continuation of distribution assured a Tyler veto, and much political advantage for the Whigs. Once Tyler vetoed it, a House committee headed by Massachusetts' <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a> condemned his actions. Fillmore prepared a second bill, this time omitting distribution, and when it reached his desk, Tyler signed it, but in the process offended his erstwhile Democratic allies. Thus, Fillmore not only achieved his legislative goal, but managed to politically isolate Tyler.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback1957.E2.80.932186_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback1957.E2.80.932186-54">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fillmore received praise for the tariff, but in July 1842 he announced he would not seek re-election. The Whigs nominated him anyway, but he refused it. Tired of Washington life and the conflict that had revolved around President Tyler, Fillmore sought to return to his life and law practice in Buffalo. Fillmore continued to be active in the <a href="/wiki/Lame_duck_session" class="mw-redirect" title="Lame duck session">lame duck session</a> of Congress that followed the 1842 elections and returned to Buffalo in April 1843. According to his biographer, Scarry: "Fillmore concluded his Congressional career at a point when he had become a powerful figure, an able statesman at the height of his popularity".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry1729.E2.80.931776_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry1729.E2.80.931776-55">[53]</a></sup> Thurlow Weed deemed Congressman Fillmore "able in debate, wise in council, and inflexible in his political sentiments".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry1766_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry1766-56">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="National_figure">National figure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Millard_Fillmore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: National figure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Unidentified_Artist_-_Millard_Fillmore_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Unidentified_Artist_-_Millard_Fillmore_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-Unidentified_Artist_-_Millard_Fillmore_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="170" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Unidentified_Artist_-_Millard_Fillmore_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-Unidentified_Artist_-_Millard_Fillmore_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Unidentified_Artist_-_Millard_Fillmore_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-Unidentified_Artist_-_Millard_Fillmore_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4774" data-file-height="5745" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Unidentified_Artist_-_Millard_Fillmore_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fillmore in 1843</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Out of office, Fillmore continued his law practice and made long-neglected repairs to his Buffalo home. He remained a major political figure, leading the committee of notables that welcomed John Quincy Adams to Buffalo, and the former president expressed his regret at Fillmore's absence from the halls of Congress. Some urged Fillmore to run for vice president with Clay, the consensus Whig choice for president in 1844—<a href="/wiki/Horace_Greeley" title="Horace Greeley">Horace Greeley</a> wrote privately that "my own first choice has long been Millard Fillmore"—others thought Fillmore should try to win back the governor's mansion for the Whigs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry1776.E2.80.931820_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry1776.E2.80.931820-57">[55]</a></sup> Fillmore wanted the vice presidency, and it did not take him long after he came back from Washington for him to seek to return there in that capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback2417_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback2417-58">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fillmore hoped to gain the endorsement of the New York delegation to the national convention, but Weed wanted the vice presidency for Seward, with Fillmore as governor. Seward, however, withdrew prior to the <a href="/wiki/1844_Whig_National_Convention" title="1844 Whig National Convention">1844 Whig National Convention</a>. When Weed's replacement vice presidential hopeful, <a href="/wiki/Willis_Hall_(New_York)" title="Willis Hall (New York)">Willis Hall</a>, fell ill, Weed sought to defeat Fillmore's candidacy to force him to run for governor. Weed's attempts to boost Fillmore as a gubernatorial candidate caused the former congressman to write, "I am not willing to be treacherously killed by this pretended kindness&#160;... do not suppose for a minute that I think they desire my nomination for governor."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback2425.E2.80.932471_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback2425.E2.80.932471-59">[57]</a></sup> New York sent a delegation to Baltimore pledged to support Clay, but with no instructions as to how to vote for vice president. Weed told out-of-state delegates that the New York party preferred to have Fillmore as its gubernatorial candidate, and after Clay was nominated for president, the second place on the ticket fell to former New Jersey senator <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Frelinghuysen" title="Theodore Frelinghuysen">Theodore Frelinghuysen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback2471.E2.80.932486_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback2471.E2.80.932486-60">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Putting a good face on his defeat, Fillmore met and publicly appeared with Frelinghuysen, quietly spurning Weed's offer to get him nominated as governor at the state convention. Fillmore's position in opposing slavery, but feeling the government lacked power to abolish it, made him acceptable as a statewide Whig candidate, and Weed saw to it the pressure on Fillmore increased. Fillmore had previously stated that a convention had the right to draft anyone for political service, and Weed got the convention to choose Fillmore, who had broad support despite his reluctance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback2486.E2.80.932536_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback2486.E2.80.932536-61">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Democrats nominated Senator <a href="/wiki/Silas_Wright" title="Silas Wright">Silas Wright</a> as their gubernatorial candidate, and former Tennessee governor <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a> for president. Although Fillmore worked to gain support among <a href="/wiki/German-Americans" class="mw-redirect" title="German-Americans">German-Americans</a>, a major constituency, he was hurt among immigrants by the fact that New York City Whigs had supported a nativist candidate in the mayoral election earlier in 1844 Fillmore and his party were tarred with that brush.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback2536.E2.80.932562_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback2536.E2.80.932562-62">[60]</a></sup> He was not friendly to immigrants, and after his defeat blamed it on "foreign Catholics".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman24_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman24-63">[61]</a></sup> Clay was beaten as well.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback2486.E2.80.932536_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback2486.E2.80.932536-61">[59]</a></sup> Fillmore's biographer Paul Finkelman suggested that the hostility to immigrants and weak position on slavery defeated him for governor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman23.E2.80.9324_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman23.E2.80.9324-64">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1846, Fillmore was involved in the founding of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Buffalo" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Buffalo">University of Buffalo</a>, and became its first chancellor; he served until his death in 1874. He had opposed the <a href="/wiki/Annexation_of_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="Annexation of Texas">annexation of Texas</a>, and spoke against the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Mexican-American_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Mexican-American War">Mexican-American War</a>, seeing it as a contrivance to extend slavery's realm. Fillmore was angered when President Polk vetoed a river and harbors bill that would have benefitted Buffalo,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman35.2C_152_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman35.2C_152-65">[63]</a></sup> and wrote, "May God save the country for it is evident the people will not".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback2620_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback2620-66">[64]</a></sup> New York governors at the time served a two-year term, and Fillmore could have had the Whig nomination in 1846, had he wanted it. He actually came within one vote of it while maneuvering to get the nomination for his supporter, <a href="/wiki/John_Young_(governor)" title="John Young (governor)">John Young</a>, who was elected. A new constitution for New York state provided that the office of <a href="/wiki/Comptroller_of_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Comptroller of New York">comptroller</a> was made elective, as were the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Attorney_General" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Attorney General">attorney general</a> and some other positions that were formerly chosen by the state legislature. Fillmore's work in finance while Ways and Means chairman made him an obvious candidate, and he was successful in getting the Whig nomination for the 1847 election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback2735.E2.80.932763_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback2735.E2.80.932763-67">[65]</a></sup> With a united party at his back, Fillmore won by 38,000 votes, the largest margin a Whig candidate for statewide office would ever have in New York.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman25_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman25-68">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before moving to Albany to take office on January 1, 1848, he left his law firm and rented his house. Fillmore received positive reviews for his service as comptroller. In that office, he was a member of the state canal board, and both supported expansion and saw to it that it was managed competently. He secured an enlargement of Buffalo's canal facilities. The comptroller regulated the banks, and Fillmore stabilized the currency by requiring that state-chartered banks keep New York and federal bonds to the value of the banknotes they issued. <a href="/wiki/National_Bank_Act" title="National Bank Act">A similar plan</a> would be adopted by Congress in 1864.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback2769.E2.80.932799_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback2769.E2.80.932799-69">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1848">Election of 1848</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Millard_Fillmore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Election of 1848">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1848" title="United States presidential election, 1848">United States presidential election, 1848</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomination">Nomination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Millard_Fillmore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Nomination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MFillmore-congressman.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/MFillmore-congressman.jpg/170px-MFillmore-congressman.jpg" width="170" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/MFillmore-congressman.jpg/255px-MFillmore-congressman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/MFillmore-congressman.jpg/340px-MFillmore-congressman.jpg 2x" data-file-width="692" data-file-height="793" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MFillmore-congressman.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Engraving of Fillmore</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For further information on the procedures of American political conventions, see <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_nominating_convention" title="United States presidential nominating convention">United States presidential nominating convention</a>.</div>
<p>President Polk had pledged not to seek a second term, and with gains in Congress during the 1846 election cycle, the Whigs were hopeful of taking the White House in 1848. The party's perennial candidates, Henry Clay and Daniel Webster, each wanted the nomination, and amassed support from congressional colleagues. Many rank and file Whigs backed the Mexican War hero, General <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">Zachary Taylor</a>, for president. Although Taylor was extremely popular, many northerners had qualms about electing a Louisiana slaveholder at a time of sectional tension over whether slavery should be allowed in the territories ceded by Mexico. Taylor's uncertain political views gave others pause—career Army, he had never cast a ballot for president, though he stated that he was a Whig supporter, and some feared they might elect another Tyler, or another Harrison.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman43.E2.80.9345_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman43.E2.80.9345-70">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the nomination undecided, Weed maneuvered for New York to send an uncommitted delegation to the <a href="/wiki/1848_Whig_National_Convention" title="1848 Whig National Convention">1848 Whig National Convention</a> in Philadelphia, hoping to be a kingmaker in position to place former governor Seward on the ticket, or to get him high national office. He persuaded Fillmore to support an uncommitted ticket, though he did not tell the Buffaloan of his hopes for Seward. Weed was an influential editor, and Fillmore tended to cooperate with him for the greater good of the Whig Party. But Weed had sterner opponents, including Governor Young, who disliked Seward and did not want to see him gain high office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback2902.E2.80.932955_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback2902.E2.80.932955-71">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite Weed's efforts, Taylor was nominated on the fourth ballot, to the anger of Clay's supporters and of <a href="/wiki/Conscience_Whigs" title="Conscience Whigs">Conscience Whigs</a> from the Northeast. When order was restored, <a href="/wiki/John_A._Collier" title="John A. Collier">John A. Collier</a>, a New Yorker and a Weed opponent, addressed the convention. Delegates hung on his every word as he described himself as a Clay partisan; he had voted for Clay on each ballot. He eloquently described the grief of the Clay supporters, frustrated again in their battle to make Clay president. Collier warned of a fatal breach in the party, and stated that only one thing could prevent it: the nomination of Fillmore for vice president, whom he incorrectly depicted as a strong Clay supporter. Fillmore in fact agreed with many of Clay's positions, but did not back him for president and was not in Philadelphia. Delegates did not know this was false, or at least greatly exaggerated, and there was a large reaction in Fillmore's favor. At the time, the presidential candidate did not automatically pick his running mate, and despite the efforts of Taylor's managers to get the nomination for their choice, <a href="/wiki/Abbott_Lawrence" title="Abbott Lawrence">Abbott Lawrence</a> of Massachusetts, Fillmore became the Whig nominee for vice president on the second ballot.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback2981.E2.80.932994_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback2981.E2.80.932994-72">[70]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TaylorFillmore.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/TaylorFillmore.jpg/275px-TaylorFillmore.jpg" width="275" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/TaylorFillmore.jpg/413px-TaylorFillmore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/TaylorFillmore.jpg/550px-TaylorFillmore.jpg 2x" data-file-width="617" data-file-height="439" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TaylorFillmore.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Taylor/Fillmore campaign banner by <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Currier" title="Nathaniel Currier">Nathaniel Currier</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Weed had wanted the vice presidential nomination for Seward (who attracted few delegate votes), and Collier had acted to frustrate them in more ways than one, for with the New Yorker Fillmore as vice president, under the political customs of the time, no one from that state could be named to the cabinet. Fillmore was accused of complicity in Collier's actions, but this was never substantiated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback3001.E2.80.933008_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback3001.E2.80.933008-73">[71]</a></sup> Nevertheless, there were sound reasons for the selection of Fillmore, as he was a proven vote-getter from electorally crucial New York, and his track record in Congress and as a candidate showed his devotion to Whig doctrine, allaying fears he might be another Tyler were something to happen to General Taylor. Delegates remembered him for his role in the Tariff of 1842, and he had been mentioned as a vice presidential possibility along with Lawrence and Ohio's <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Ewing" title="Thomas Ewing">Thomas Ewing</a>. His rivalry with Seward (already known for anti-slavery views and statements) made him more acceptable in the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman47.E2.80.9349_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman47.E2.80.9349-74">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESnyder37_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnyder37-75">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_election_campaign">General election campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Millard_Fillmore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: General election campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It was customary in mid-19th century America for a candidate for high office not to appear to seek it. Thus, Fillmore remained at the comptroller's office in Albany, and made no speeches; the 1848 campaign was conducted in the newspapers and with addresses made by surrogates at rallies. The Democrats nominated Michigan Senator <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Cass" title="Lewis Cass">Lewis Cass</a> for president, with General <a href="/wiki/William_O._Butler" class="mw-redirect" title="William O. Butler">William O. Butler</a> his running mate, but it would be a three-way fight as the <a href="/wiki/Free_Soil_Party" title="Free Soil Party">Free Soil Party</a>, opposed to the spread of slavery, chose former president Van Buren.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry3138.E2.80.933150_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry3138.E2.80.933150-76">[74]</a></sup> There was a crisis among the Whigs when Taylor also accepted the presidential nomination of a group of dissident South Carolina Democrats. Fearing that Taylor would be an party apostate like Tyler, Weed in late August scheduled a rally in Albany aimed at electing an uncommitted slate of presidential electors, but Fillmore interceded with the editor, assuring him that Taylor was loyal to the party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman53_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman53-77">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry3188.E2.80.933245_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry3188.E2.80.933245-78">[76]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1848.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/ElectoralCollege1848.svg/220px-ElectoralCollege1848.svg.png" width="220" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/ElectoralCollege1848.svg/330px-ElectoralCollege1848.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/ElectoralCollege1848.svg/440px-ElectoralCollege1848.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1020" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1848.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Results by state. Those won by Taylor and Fillmore are in yellow.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Northerners assumed that Fillmore, hailing from a free state, was an opponent of the spread of slavery. Southerners accused him of being an abolitionist, which he hotly denied.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman51_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman51-79">[77]</a></sup> Fillmore responded to one Alabaman in a widely published letter that slavery was an evil, but one that the federal government had no authority over.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman53_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman53-77">[75]</a></sup> Taylor and Fillmore corresponded twice in September, with the general happy that the crisis over the South Carolinians was resolved. Fillmore, for his part, assured his running mate that the electoral prospects for the ticket looked good, especially in the Northeast.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry3245.E2.80.933258_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry3245.E2.80.933258-80">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the end, the Taylor/Fillmore ticket won narrowly, with New York's electoral votes again key to the election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback3090_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback3090-81">[79]</a></sup> The Whig ticket won the popular vote by 1,361,393 (47.3 percent) to 1,223,460 (42.5 percent), and triumphed 163 to 127 in the Electoral College.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[c]</a></sup> Minor party candidates took no electoral votes,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry3283_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry3283-83">[80]</a></sup> but the strength of the burgeoning anti-slavery movement was shown by the vote for Van Buren, who though he won no states earned 291,501 votes (10.1 percent) and finished second in New York, Vermont and Massachusetts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman51.E2.80.9352_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman51.E2.80.9352-84">[81]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vice_president_.281849.E2.80.931850.29">Vice president (1849–1850)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Millard_Fillmore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Vice president (1849–1850)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Millard_Fillmore_daguerreotype_by_Mathew_Brady_1849-crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Millard_Fillmore_daguerreotype_by_Mathew_Brady_1849-crop.jpg/170px-Millard_Fillmore_daguerreotype_by_Mathew_Brady_1849-crop.jpg" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Millard_Fillmore_daguerreotype_by_Mathew_Brady_1849-crop.jpg/255px-Millard_Fillmore_daguerreotype_by_Mathew_Brady_1849-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Millard_Fillmore_daguerreotype_by_Mathew_Brady_1849-crop.jpg/340px-Millard_Fillmore_daguerreotype_by_Mathew_Brady_1849-crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="424" data-file-height="595" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Millard_Fillmore_daguerreotype_by_Mathew_Brady_1849-crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fillmore in 1849</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1850" title="Compromise of 1850">Compromise of 1850</a></div>
<p>Millard Fillmore was sworn in as vice president on March 5, 1849, in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Senate_Chamber" title="Old Senate Chamber">Senate Chamber</a>. As March 4, then the usual <a href="/wiki/Inauguration_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="Inauguration Day">Inauguration Day</a>, fell on a Sunday, the swearing-in was postponed until the following day. Fillmore took the oath from <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/Roger_B._Taney" title="Roger B. Taney">Roger B. Taney</a> and in turn swore in the senators beginning their terms, including Seward, who in February had <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_election_in_New_York,_1849" title="United States Senate election in New York, 1849">been elected by the New York legislature</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[d]</a></sup> Fillmore then went outside with the senators to attend <a href="/wiki/Inauguration_of_Zachary_Taylor" title="Inauguration of Zachary Taylor">Taylor's inauguration</a>, and that night accompanied the president to the <a href="/wiki/Inaugural_ball" class="mw-redirect" title="Inaugural ball">inaugural balls</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESnyder39.E2.80.9341_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnyder39.E2.80.9341-86">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fillmore had spent the four months between the election and swearing-in being feted by the New York Whigs and winding up affairs in the comptroller's office. Taylor had written promising influence in the new administration, but the <a href="/wiki/President-elect_of_the_United_States" title="President-elect of the United States">president-elect</a> mistakenly thought that the vice president was a cabinet member, which was not true in the 19th century. Fillmore, Seward and Weed had met and come to general agreement on how to divide federal jobs in New York. Seward, once he went to Washington, made friendly contact with Taylor's cabinet nominees, advisers, and the general's brother, and an alliance between the incoming administration and the Weed machine was soon under way behind Fillmore's back. In exchange for support, Seward and Weed were allowed to designate who would fill federal jobs in New York, with Fillmore given far less than had been agreed. When Fillmore, after the inauguration, discovered this, he went to Taylor, but the only result was that the warfare against Fillmore's influence became open. Fillmore supporters like Collier, who had nominated him at the convention, were passed over for candidates backed by Weed, who was triumphant even in Buffalo. This greatly increased the influence of Weed in New York politics, and diminished Fillmore's. According to Rayback, "by mid-1849, Fillmore's situation had become desperate."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback3101.E2.80.933307_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback3101.E2.80.933307-88">[84]</a></sup> Despite his lack of influence, he was pestered by office seekers and those seeking to lease or sell a house to him, as there was then no official residence for the vice president. He enjoyed one aspect of his office, due to his lifelong love of learning: he became deeply involved in the administration of the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a> as a member <i><a href="/wiki/Ex_officio" class="mw-redirect" title="Ex officio">ex officio</a></i> of its Board of Regents.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith160.E2.80.93162_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith160.E2.80.93162-89">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Through 1849, the status of slavery in the territories remained unresolved. Taylor advocated the admission of California and of New Mexico<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[e]</a></sup> as states; both were likely to outlaw slavery. Southerners were surprised to learn the president, despite being a Southern slaveholder, did not support the introduction of slavery into the new territories, as he believed the institution could not flourish in the arid Southwest. There was anger across party lines in the South, where making the territories free of slavery was considered excluding Southerners from part of the national heritage. When Congress met in December 1849, this discord broke out in the election for Speaker, which took weeks and dozens of ballots to resolve as the House divided along sectional lines.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback3307.E2.80.933367_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback3307.E2.80.933367-91">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith93.E2.80.9394_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith93.E2.80.9394-92">[87]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_Clay_Senate3_crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Henry_Clay_Senate3_crop.jpg/300px-Henry_Clay_Senate3_crop.jpg" width="300" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Henry_Clay_Senate3_crop.jpg/450px-Henry_Clay_Senate3_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Henry_Clay_Senate3_crop.jpg/600px-Henry_Clay_Senate3_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2096" data-file-height="1868" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_Clay_Senate3_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
From a <a href="/wiki/Peter_F._Rothermel" title="Peter F. Rothermel">Peter F. Rothermel</a> engraving: Vice President Fillmore (upper right) presides over the Compromise debates as <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> takes the floor of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Senate_Chamber" title="Old Senate Chamber">Old Senate Chamber</a>. <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a> (seen in part standing just to Fillmore's right) and <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Webster" title="Daniel Webster">Daniel Webster</a> (seated to the left of Clay) look on.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Fillmore fought back against Weed by building a network of like-minded Whigs in New York state, with their positions publicized by the establishment of a rival newspaper to Weed's <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Albany_Evening_Journal&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Albany Evening Journal (page does not exist)">Albany Evening Journal</a></i>. This was backed by wealthy New Yorkers. All pretense at friendship between Fillmore and Weed vanished in November 1849 when the two happened to meet in New York City, and they exchanged accusations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERayback3367.E2.80.933399_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERayback3367.E2.80.933399-93">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fillmore presided<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[f]</a></sup> over some of the most momentous and passionate debates in American history as the Senate debated whether to allow slavery in the territories. The ongoing sectional conflict had already excited much discussion when on January 21, 1850, President Taylor sent a special message to Congress urging the admission of California immediately and New Mexico later, and that the Supreme Court settle the boundary dispute whereby the state of Texas claimed much of what is now the state of New Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScarry3445.E2.80.933467_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarry3445.E2.80.933467-95">[89]</a></sup> On January 29, Henry Clay introduced what was called the "<a href="/wiki/Omnibus_bill" title="Omnibus bill">Omnibus Bill</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[g]</a></sup> The bill would give victories to both North and South: it would admit California as a free state, organize territorial governments in New Mexico and <a href="/wiki/Utah_Territory" title="Utah Territory">Utah</a>, and ban the importation of slaves into the District of Columbia for sale and export out of it. It would also toughen the <a href="/wiki/Fugitive_Slave_Act_(1793)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fugitive Slave Act (1793)">Fugitive Slave Act</a>, as resistance to enforcement in parts of the North was a longtime Southern grievance. Clay's bill provided for the settlement of the Texas-New Mexico boundary dispute; the status of slavery in the territories would be decided by those living there (known as <a href="/wiki/Popular_sovereignty" title="Popular sovereignty">popular sovereignty</a>). Taylor was unenthusiastic about the bill, and it languished in Congress, but Fillmore, after hearing weeks of debate, in May 1850 informed Taylor that if senators divided equally on the bill, he would cast his tie-breaking vote in favor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmerican_National_Biography_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmerican_National_Biography-4">[2]</a></sup> He did his best to keep the peace among the senators, reminding them of the vice president's power to rule them out of order, but was blamed for failing to maintain it when a physical confrontation between Mississippi's <a href="/wiki/Henry_S._Foote" title="Henry S. Foote">Henry S. Foote</a> and Missouri's <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hart_Benton_(politician)" title="Thomas Hart Benton (politician)">Thomas Hart Benton</a> broke out on April 17, with Foote pointing a gun at his colleague as Benton advanced on him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith138.E2.80.93139.2C_163.E2.80.93165_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith138.E2.80.93139.2C_163.E2.80.93165-97">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_.281850.E2.80.931853.29">Presidency (1850–1853)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Millard_Fillmore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Presidency (1850–1853)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Millard_Fillmore" title="Presidency of Millard Fillmore">Presidency of Millard Fillmore</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="width: 230px; height: 295px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -50px; left: -43px; width: 325px"><a href="/wiki/File:FILLMORE,_Millard-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Fillmore as president" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/FILLMORE%2C_Millard-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/325px-FILLMORE%2C_Millard-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="325" height="427" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/FILLMORE%2C_Millard-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/488px-FILLMORE%2C_Millard-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/FILLMORE%2C_Millard-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/650px-FILLMORE%2C_Millard-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8050" data-file-height="10568" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FILLMORE,_Millard-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:FILLMORE, Millard-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:FILLMORE, Millard-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Fillmore as president</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Succession_amid_crisis">Succession amid crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Millard_Fillmore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Succession amid crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>July 4, 1850 was a very hot day in Washington, and <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">President Taylor</a>, who attended <a href="/wiki/Fourth_of_July" class="mw-redirect" title="Fourth of July">Fourth of July</a> ceremonies, refreshed himself, likely with cold milk and cherries. What he consumed probably gave him <a href="/wiki/Gastroenteritis" title="Gastroenteritis">gastroenteritis</a>, and he died on July 9. Taylor, nicknamed "Old Rough and Ready", had gained a reputation for toughness through his military campaigning in the heat, and his sudden death came as a shock to the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman1_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman1-98">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fillmore had been called from his chair presiding over the Senate on July 8, and had sat with members of the cabinet in a vigil outside Taylor's bedroom at the White House. He received the formal notification of the president's death, signed by the cabinet, on the evening of July 9 in his residence at the <a href="/wiki/Willard_Hotel" class="mw-redirect" title="Willard Hotel">Willard Hotel</a>. After acknowledging the letter, and spending a sleepless night,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESnyder43_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnyder43-99">[92]</a></sup> Fillmore went to the House of Representatives, where, at a <a href="/wiki/Joint_session_of_the_United_States_Congress" title="Joint session of the United States Congress">joint session of Congress</a>, he took the oath as president from <a href="/wiki/William_Cranch" title="William Cranch">William Cranch</a>, chief judge of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Circuit_Court_of_the_District_of_Columbia" title="United States Circuit Court of the District of Columbia">the federal court for the District of Columbia</a>, and the man who had sworn in President Tyler. The cabinet officers, as was customary when a new president took over, submitted their resignations, expecting Fillmore to refuse, allowing them to continue in office. Fillmore had been marginalized by the cabinet members, and the new president accepted the resignations, though he asked them to stay on for a month, which most refused to do. Fillmore is the only president who succeeded by death or resignation not to retain, at least initially, his predecessor's cabinet. He was already in discussions with Whig leaders, and on July 20 began to send new nominations to the Senate, with the Fillmore cabinet to be led by Webster as Secretary of State. Webster had outraged his Massachusetts constituents by supporting Clay's bill, and with his Senate term to expire in 1851, had no electoral future in his home state. Fillmore appointed his old law partner, Nathan Hall, as <a href="/wiki/Postmaster_General_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Postmaster General of the United States">Postmaster General</a>, a cabinet position that controlled many patronage appointments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkelman72.E2.80.9377_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkelman72.E2.80.9377-100">[93]</a></sup> The new department heads were mostly supporters of the Compromise, as was Fillmore.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenstein_.26_Anderson48_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenstein_.26_Anderson48-101">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>The brief pause from politics out of national grief at Taylor's death did not abate the crisis. Texas had attempted to assert its authority in New Mexico territory, and the state's governor, <a href="/wiki/Peter_H._Bell" class="mw-redirect" title="Peter H. Bell">Peter H. Bell</a>, had sent belligerent letters to President Taylor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith152.E2.80.93157_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith152.E2.80.93157-102">[95]</a></sup> Fillmore received another such after becoming president.
